    Help

    edX Help

    For questions on course lectures, homework, tools, or materials for this course, post in the course discussion forum.

    Have general questions about edX? You can find lots of helpful information in the edX FAQ.

    Have a question about something specific? You can contact the edX general support team directly:

    Report a problem Make a suggestion Ask a question

    Please note: The edX support team is English speaking. While we will do our best to address your inquiry in any language, our responses will be in English.

    Thank You!

    Thank you for your inquiry or feedback. We typically respond to a request within one business day (Monday to Friday, 13:00 UTC to 21:00 UTC.) In the meantime, please review our detailed FAQs where most questions have already been answered.

    Complexity Classes - Time 18:57

    2. Eric Grimson: We're now going to take the ideas we just saw and look at the
    3. different instances of different complexity classes.
    4. So what are those ideas?
    5. Given an algorithm, we want to count what are the number of basic steps
    6. it's going to need.
    7. We want to count those as a function of the size of the problem.
    8. And we're only going to worry about the case when the size of that problem
    9. tends to get large.
    10. What is the asymptotic behavior as that happens?
    11. We're going to see, in this segment, a set of examples of different
    12. complexity classes.
    13. We're going to see constant time algorithms, referred to as O(1).
    14. We're going to see logarithmic.
    15. We're going to see linear.
    16. We're going to see log-linear, or n times log n.
    17. We're going to see polynomial, and we're going to see exponential
    18. algorithms.
    19. Let me say a word about those last two because they are actually different,
    20. and sometimes people get confused by them.
    21. The polynomial time algorithm is something whose number of steps
    22. asymptotically is n, where n is the size of the problem, to some constant.
    23. So a quadratic algorithm is n^2, a cubic algorithm is n^3.
    24. An exponential algorithm is one where the number of basic steps
    25. asymptotically is described by some constant to n,
    26. the size of the problem.
    27. So that could be 2^n.
    28. And keep in mind that n^2 grows in a very different manner than 2^n,
    29. as we change n.
    30. And in fact, this sequence is really listed in roughly the order of
    31. increasing complexity.
    32. Each one of these classes describes a slightly more
    33. complex kind of algorithm.
    34. And as a consequence, we tend to want to be as high up in
    35. this chain as we can.
    36. If I have two algorithms that solve the same problem, one of them is
    37. quadratic, the other one is linear, I want the linear one because it's
    38. almost certainly going to be more efficient.
    39. If I've got an algorithm that is log-linear, n log n, and another one
    40. that is just logarithmic, I want the log one because it's
    41. always going to be better.
    42. Finally, we tend to be a little sloppy in this subject in terms of how we use
    43. these terms.
    44. Really what we're talking about here are upper bounds on the complexity of
    45. the algorithm, on the number of steps.
    46. In many cases, it's an actual tight bound, it's both an
    47. upper and lower bound.
    48. But if we're not certain about an algorithm, we may end up saying, for
    49. example, that it's exponential because the best analysis we can do says that
    50. the running time may involve an exponential number of steps.
    51. It may be possible to show that it's less than that, but if we can't
    52. actually do it, we're going to use the upper bound to capture the class.
    53. With that, let's start looking at some examples.
    54. So the first class is constant complexity.
    55. That says the complexity is independent of the size of the input.
    56. This is great, and unfortunately, there aren't very many interesting
    57. algorithms in this class.
    58. It says, literally, that the number of steps doesn't depend on the size of
    59. the algorithm.
    60. Now while pieces of code may satisfy that, it's not common for an entire
    61. algorithm to have that property.
    62. If you've got one, though, still great.
    63. And note, by the way, that even in this case, you can have loops or
    64. recursive calls, it's just that the number of iterations, through the
    65. loop, or the number of recursive calls, is independent of
    66. the size of the input.
    67. All right.
    68. Let's move on, though, to more interesting ones.
    69. Logarithmic algorithms.
    70. Here, the complexity of the algorithm, the number of steps, grows as the log
    71. of the size of one of its inputs.
    72. And you've actually seen a great example of this, bisection search.
    73. Remember when we were looking for square roots?
    74. We said start between 0 and x, take the midpoint.
    75. If that's close enough, we're done.
    76. If not, depending on the size, either we're going to look only in the bottom
    77. range or in the top range.
    78. Again, we take the input, the midpoint.
    79. And either we look at the bottom range or the top range.
    80. At each step we're cutting down the set of possibilities of which we need
    81. to look by a factor of two.
    82. And that cutting down a problem in half, constantly, is a great
    83. indication of, in fact, a logarithmic algorithm.
    84. So bisection search is a great example.
    85. You can do the same idea, by the way, on a list.
    86. If I have a list of elements that are in order, and I want to decide if an
    87. element's in that list, use the same idea.
    88. Look at the midpoint.
    89. If I happen to get lucky, I'm done.
    90. If not, based on the size, either I'll just look at the bottom half of the
    91. list or the top half of the list.
    92. So that kind of property is indicative of a logarithmic search, the
    93. logarithmic algorithm.
    94. Now, let me show you an example of a piece of code doing this, just to show
    95. you how we would reason about it to decide whether something's
    96. logarithmic.
    97. This is a little piece of code to convert an integer into a string.
    98. Don't worry about the fact that Python will do this for you automatically.
    99. Let's just see how the algorithm shows logarithmic complexity.
    100. I'm going to set up an internal variable called digits.
    101. It's just the string of all the digits.
    102. And then given i, if it's 0, I'm done.
    103. If not, what am I going to do?
    104. I'm going to set up a little variable result that's
    105. initially an empty string.
    106. And then I'm going to take that integer, i, and the first thing I'm
    107. going to do is I'm going to take the remainder, which basically pulls off
    108. the bottom digit.
    109. I'm going to index in two digits to find the corresponding string
    110. representation.
    111. And then I'm just going to add that to the front of result, putting it in the
    112. right spot on the thing that I'm accumulating.
    113. And having done that, I'm going to divide i by 10, which throws away the
    114. bottom digit, shifts everything to the left, or to the right rather.
    115. Then I keep doing that.
    116. Now if I were to count steps here, what really matters is that loop.
    117. And in fact, let's look at that.
    118. I only have to look at the loop because there are no
    119. function calls here.
    120. And within the loop, how many steps do I have?
    121. Well, I've got the remainder, that's 1.
    122. I've got the add, that's 2.
    123. I've got the look up, that's 3.
    124. I've got the assignment, that's 4.
    125. I've got the division, which is 5, and the assignment, which is 6.
    126. So there are 6 steps within the loop.
    127. But it doesn't depend on the size of i.
    128. It's always the same thing, 6 steps within the loop.
    129. So really what matters is how many times do I go through the loop?
    130. And for that, I have to think about, so what is changing?
    131. And what's changing is the iteration variable, or the loop variable i.
    132. And at each stage through the loop, it gets divided by 10.
    133. So how many times can I divide i by 10 before I get down to a
    134. single digit case?
    135. And the answer is a logarithmic time, so this is O(log(n)).
    136. Now let's be careful here.
    137. You might say, well, wait a minute.
    138. Why doesn't this depend on the size of the integer that I pass in there?
    139. And it depends, not on the size, but on the number of digits.
    140. Another way of saying it is-- if I have some number of steps to solve a
    141. problem with one version of i, and I take 10 times i, I'm only going to go
    142. through the loop one more time.
    143. It's the number of digits, not the size of the problem.
    144. And that's why it's logarithmic in the size of i because that log gives me
    145. the number of digits.
    146. How about linear algorithms?
    147. Well, searching a list where I just walk through it in order is a great
    148. example of a linear time algorithm.
    149. In the worst case, I've got to look at every element of the list.
    150. So it's linear in the size of the problem.
    151. Here's another example of a linear algorithm, and I want to use this to
    152. look at how we measure it.
    153. Suppose I've got a string that's assumed to be
    154. composed of decimal digits.
    155. I want to add up the characters of the string to get a number out.
    156. A trivial little thing to do, but let's look at what the
    157. code might look like.
    158. I'm going to start off with some initial, internal variable called val.
    159. And for each character in the string, I'm going to convert it into an
    160. integer and add it into what I've got in val in order to return
    161. that overall value.
    162. I don't care about the computation, what I care about is the complexity,
    163. and the complexity is pretty straightforward.
    164. I have to go through each element in the string once.
    165. So this is linear in the length of the string.
    166. Within the loop, it's a constant number of things to do.
    167. So all I care about is how many times do I go through the loop, and that is
    168. linear in the size of the problem.
    169. So far we've seen examples where we're just looking at things that have
    170. loops, and we're worrying about how many times does it
    171. go through the loop.
    172. We can also have a complexity that depends on the number of recursive
    173. calls, and our good friend factorial is a great example.
    174. There's the code for factorial, you've seen it before.
    175. If I think about the complexity of this,
    176. I could say, well, within the actual code itself, there's only a constant
    177. number of things to do.
    178. I'm doing a test.
    179. If the test is true, I'm just returning something.
    180. Otherwise, I'm doing a multiplication and a recursive call, but in the body
    181. of the procedure itself, it's a constant number of things.
    182. So what matters here is, how many times do I call factorial?
    183. And we can see that on each recursive call, we're reducing n by 1.
    184. So I've got to do fact(n), then fact(n-1), then fact(n-2).
    185. And you can see that I'm going to call factorial n times.
    186. Within each call, complexity's constant so, therefore, this is linear
    187. in the size of the problem because there's a linear number
    188. of recursive calls.
    189. Algorithms with this kind of template are typically linear, and that's a
    190. handy thing to know.
    191. What about log-linear?
    192. Well, a lot of practical algorithms fall into this category.
    193. They're n times the logarithm of n.
    194. In fact, an example we're going to see later on is one of the most
    195. commonly-used log-linear algorithms, which is an algorithm called merge
    196. sort, which is a way of sorting a list to get it into increasing or
    197. decreasing order.
    198. I just want to highlight that we have algorithms of this sort.
    199. We're going to come back to merge sort later on to see how we can do
    200. reasoning to find these kinds of examples.
    201. So let's move on to polynomial.
    202. Polynomial complexity says I've got an algorithm that grows
    203. as n to some power.
    204. And most common polynomial algorithms are, in fact, quadratic.
    205. They grow as n squared or some parameter, some
    206. size parameter, squared.
    207. And these commonly occur when we have nested loops or when we have
    208. particular kinds of recursive function calls.
    209. So let's look at an example, and this is probably a good example of a very
    210. common kind of quadratic complexity.
    211. This is a little algorithm that's going to decide whether, given two
    212. lists, one of them is a subset of the other.
    213. And that says-- is every element of L1 in L2?
    214. The code to do this we can describe pretty quickly.
    215. We're going to loop over every element in L1.
    216. We're going to set up, each time through the loop, to say a variable
    217. match is equal to false.
    218. And what am I going to do?
    219. I'm going to loop over every element in L2, or almost every element in L2,
    220. looking to see if I find one that's equal to e1.
    221. If I find something that's equal, I set the flag to True and I break.
    222. And what that break does is it throws us out of this loop, this interior
    223. loop, which means I can then skip down to, basically, this step here.
    224. And if, in fact, matched is False, I return False.
    225. But if it's True, then I simply skip back around.
    226. OK?
    227. And that then says I'm going to basically run through this loop until,
    228. either I go all the way through, in which case matched is False because I
    229. didn't find an instance of it, or I do find an instance, and I break out of
    230. it and I set a value that returns.
    231. All right.
    232. I'm going to do that over all versions of this outer loop, e1.
    233. So let's look at an example to see how we'd measure the complexity here.
    234. The outer loop is executed the length of L1 times, so however long L1 is,
    235. I'm going to do that outer loop that many times.
    236. Each iteration is going to execute the inner loop up to the
    237. length of L2 times.
    238. I have to go all the way through L2, the length of L2.
    239. If I find something earlier, I break out of it.
    240. So, in the worst case, this has a complexity that's the product of
    241. length of L1 times length of L2.
    242. Now, that depends on L1 and L2.
    243. What Is the actual worst case?
    244. Well, the worst case is when they're both of the same size.
    245. L1 can't be bigger than L2 or it wouldn't be a subset.
    246. When they're of the same size, but none of the elements of L1 are in L2,
    247. then I'm going to run through this loop the full number of times.
    248. I'm going to run through it L2 times.
    249. In that case, the complexity is quadratic.
    250. It's the length of L1 squared.
    251. It's quadratic in that size.
    252. Let's look at another example.
    253. Here's another example involving two lists, and I want to find the
    254. intersection of those two lists.
    255. And here I want to show how, having multiple loops, we're going to focus
    256. in on the one with the worst complexity.
    257. The idea here is I want to take two lists, L1 and L2.
    258. I'm going to set up an initial temporary list, and then I'm going to
    259. loop over everything in L1.
    260. And for every element of L2, I'm going to look to see--
    261. is it equal to the thing I'm looking for in L1?
    262. And if it is, I'm going to add it into tmp.
    263. Now because L2 might have multiple instances of something, I
    264. want to keep going.
    265. So even if I find an instance of e1 in L2, I'm going to go all the way
    266. through L2 and gather each of those instances together.
    267. I'm going to do that over all possible versions of e1.
    268. And when I'm done, I need to now strip away the duplicates.
    269. So I'm going to set up another temporary variable, res.
    270. And then I'm going to loop over that list I just built, tmp, and basically
    271. say-- if e is not in res, then I'm going to add it in.
    272. But if I've already got a version of it there, I'm simply going to ignore
    273. it and keep going.
    274. So that, ultimately, it'll return a list of unique elements that are those
    275. that appear in both lists.
    276. Again, what's the complexity here?
    277. Well to see this, we can note that this first nested loop up here is very
    278. similar to what we saw before.
    279. It's going to take the product of the length of L1 and the length of L2
    280. number of steps.
    281. And, in fact, it's going to do exactly that because I go through L2 in full
    282. each time around.
    283. What about this loop?
    284. There's a second loop there, I have to think about that.
    285. Well, that second loop takes, at most, the length of L1 number of steps
    286. because I'm simply going through that list once.
    287. So I've now got two terms.
    288. I've got a quadratic term, and I've got a linear term.
    289. And, of course, we know that the quadratic term really dominates here.
    290. It's the big one.
    291. It's the one that really matters.
    292. So I can ignore the second term, and basically say that this is of order
    293. length of L1 times length of L2.
    294. So it's quadratic.
    295. The point of this is I need to look at the loops.
    296. If I have different loops, or multiple loops, within a piece of code, I need
    297. to find the one that has the higher complexity.
    298. And that will tend to be the nested one, as it was right there.
    299. OK.
    300. What about exponential algorithms?
    301. What characterizes an exponential algorithm?
    302. And an exponential algorithm, by the way, is usually a
    303. very expensive algorithm.
    304. Well, it's typically a case when we've got recursive functions where there's
    305. more than one recursive call for each step of the problem.
    306. We saw an example earlier.
    307. Towers of Hanoi has two recursive calls.
    308. I moved a smaller stack once, this moved the bottom case and then moves
    309. the smallest stack again.
    310. So I had two recursive calls per each size of the problem.
    311. And that is characteristic of an exponential problem.
    312. But unfortunately, many important problems are inherently exponential.
    313. And that's really unfortunate because the costs can be very high.
    314. And one of the things we want to see is that's going to lead us to consider
    315. finding approximate solutions in more efficient time, rather than trying to
    316. do something that deals with finding the exact solution.
    317. Nonetheless, let's do one last example here.
    318. Let's look at an example of something that is exponential in complexity, and
    319. look at how we would actually measure it.
    320. So this is an example where I've got a list of the elements, for example, it
    321. might be a list of numbers.
    322. And I want to generate another list of all of the subsets of that list.
    323. So if the list is one and two, I'd like a new list as output that has the
    324. empty list, the list of one, the list of two, an the list of one and two.
    325. So all of the different possible subsets or combinations of elements
    326. out of that list.
    327. Again, I'm not as worried about it, but I want to see how the complexity
    328. goes there.
    329. So here's what the code does.
    330. I'm going to set up a place to store my answer.
    331. If there's nothing in the list, if the list is of length 0, then I want to
    332. return a list that is just a list of the empty list.
    333. So there's this funky-looking thing right here with two square brackets.
    334. So it is a list whose only element is the empty list because that's the only
    335. possible subset of a list of length 0.
    336. Otherwise, how am I going to solve this?
    337. Well, I'm going to use that recursive idea.
    338. I'm going to first say, let's take everything in the list but the last
    339. element, and get all the possible subsets of that.
    340. I'm going to call that smaller, right here.
    341. I'm going to get a list of just the last element right
    342. there, which is extra.
    343. And here's what I want to do.
    344. I want to take all of the things in smaller, and add that last element to
    345. them because those are all subsets of the longer list.
    346. And then I want to take all of the instances, all of the subsets without
    347. the last element, and all of the subsets with the last element, and add
    348. them together.
    349. And I'm done.
    350. So there's a little loop where I take everything in the smaller one, and I
    351. add that last element on to it.
    352. And I create those into new.
    353. And then, finally, I create a flattened version, which is all of the
    354. versions without the last element, all of the versions with the last element,
    355. and I'm done.
    356. You can go code this up and try to see that it does the right thing.
    357. What I'm really interested in is the complexity.
    358. So to measure that, I'm going to assume that append is actually
    359. constant time.
    360. In fact, it is, given the implementation inside of Python, but
    361. since it's not a basic element, I need to make that assumption.
    362. And now what do I want to look at?
    363. What's the time?
    364. Well, the amount of time it's going to take here is basically going to be the
    365. amount of time it takes to solve this problem, the smaller size problem,
    366. plus the amount of time that's needed to make a copy of all the elements of
    367. the smaller problem right there.
    368. OK?
    369. So how can I get at that?
    370. Well, it's important to think about the size of that smaller problem.
    371. And, in particular, we know that, for a set of size k, there are 2^k cases.
    372. There are 2^k different possible subsets inside of there.
    373. So if I want to solve a problem of size n, I'm going to need to solve a
    374. problem of size n - 1, and there are 2^n possible solutions there.
    375. And then, in the problem of size n - 2, there are going to be 2^(n-1) and
    376. so on, all the way down to 2^0.
    377. Now we can actually solve this, but a little bit of math actually tells me
    378. that that sum, 2^0 + 2^1 + ...
    379. + 2^(n-1), is 2^n.
    380. And so the math tells us that this is a problem of size 2^n.
    381. This is important because it looks like there's just one recursive call
    382. at each step, and there is.
    383. But it's on a problem whose size is growing exponentially.
    384. And that, therefore, when I come down here, and I want to walk through that
    385. list, basically adding all of them in, that loop there has a size that's
    386. O(2^n), for whatever the length of the list L is.
    387. And as a consequence, this is an exponential-sized algorithm.
    388. OK.
    389. So what does that give us?
    390. It says we've now seen a sequence of different instances of classes of
    391. algorithms.
    392. We've seen characteristics or templates of each one of them.
    393. And we've seen, that as we go down this list, they get worse and worse.
    394. As we go through the rest of the course, we're going to see examples of
    395. this complexity.
    396. But those templates already give you a way of getting a sense of what is the
    397. complexity of a particular solution.
    Go back to start of transcript.
